The complaints I have deal with a sometimes amateurish production quality and some very contrived plot points and characterizations. Courageous is a surprisingly well made movie with a clear Christian message. Just don't expect Hollywood quality acting or dialogue from this cast and you should come away impressed by this film.

B000002GKT|5.0|English rockers Humble Pie released its fifth album Performance : Rockin' the Fillmore in November of 1971.<br />By 1971, the band Humble Pie (which consisted of former Small Faces singer/guitarist (the sadly missed) Steve Marriott, former Herd lead guitarist Peter Frampton, former Spooky Tooth bass player (the also missed) Greg Ridley and drummer Jerry Shirley) had recorded four studio albums but they only hinted at the greatness this band would unleash (especially the underground classic Rock On) but were upstaging bands they were opening for like Grand Funk Railroad, Ten Years After and Lee Michaels with their high octane performances. With Humble Pie rocking concert halls, the band recorded the Rockin' the Fillmore album shortly after The Allman Brothers Band recorded its classic breakthrough Live at Fillmore East.<br />There was one true hard rock live album before Rockin' the Fillmore and that was The Who's Live at Leeds. Then when Rockin' the Fillmore hit, it paved the way for other classic hard rock live albums like Deep Purple's Made in Japan and KISS' Alive (which wasn't the first hard rock double live album much to Gene Simmons' greedy chagrin) as I found out when I bought the CD in August of 2000 on a whim along with Thin Lizzy's Live and Dangerous.<br />We open with the great rocking \\"Four Day Creep\\" which is an old blues number rocked up in Humble Pie fashion. Then Marriott proved why he was one of the greatest rock singers with his acapella bluesy/gospel vocal counterpointing Frampton's lightning guitar riffs on the intro to \\"I'm Ready\\". This reworking of the Willie Dixon classic is arguably better than anything Zeppelin was recording. Then the only Humble Pie original on the whole album is the track \\"Stone Cold Fever\\" from Rock On and is superior to the studio version.<br />Then Side Two of the original album is taken up by a 23 and a half minute reading of the Dr John track \\"I Walk on Gilded Splinters\\" which rocks. The song starts as a slow blues then picks up steam during Peter Frampton's guitar solo and then gives way into a stellar harmonica solo by Marriott (who finally gets inducted into The Rock and Roll Hall of Fame this year (though it's for his years with The Small Faces)) then into another stellar solo from Frampton.<br />Side Three of the original album is taken up by the band's revved up 16 plus minute rendition of \\"Rolling Stone\\" (which is not neither an ode to the magazine nor the band nor is it a cover of Bob Dylan's \\"Like a Rolling Stone\\" but a cover of the late Muddy Waters' blues classic) and it just rocks (a studio version was on Rock On but this is the definitive version of the song).<br />Side Four of the original album starts with a rocking take of the late Ray Charles' \\"Halleujah (I Love Her So)\\" and just makes the song their own. We close the album with the show closer, \\"I Don't Need No Doctor\\" which was originally by the late Nick Ashford and Valerie Simpson but the band makes this song their own and would become a signature song for the band.<br />Sadly, this album would be the last with Peter Frampton who left the band during the mixing of the album in September of 1971 but we're left with a classic live album.<br />Performance : Rockin' the Fillmore reached #21 on the Billboard chart and gave the band its first Gold selling album within three months of its release. Forty years on, Rockin' the Fillmore is still a classic live album and showed us why Humble Pie and (in particular) Steve Marriott (who tragically died in a house fire while smoking in bed at the age of 44 in 1991) still sounds fresh today.<br />RECOMMENDED!m during Peter Frampton's guitar solo and then gives way into a stellar harmonica solo by Marriott (who finally gets inducted into The Rock and Roll Hall of Fame this year (though it's for his years with The Small Faces)) then into another stellar solo from Frampton.  Side Three of the original album is taken up by the band's revved up 16 plus minute rendition of \\"Rolling Stone\\" (which is not neither an ode to the magazine nor the band nor is it a cover of Bob Dylan's \\"Like a Rolling Stone\\" but a cover of the late Muddy Waters' blues classic) and it just rocks (a studio version was on Rock On but this is the definitive version of the song).  Side Four of the original album starts with a rocking take of the late Ray Charles' \\"Halleujah (I Love Her So)\\" and just makes the song their own. We close the album with the show closer, \\"I Don't Need No Doctor\\" which was originally by the late Nick Ashford and Valerie Simpson but the band makes this song their own and would become a signature song for the band.  Sadly, this album would be the last with Peter Frampton who left the band during the mixing of the album in September of 1971 but we're left with a classic live album.  Performance : Rockin' the Fillmore reached #21 on the Billboard chart and gave the band its first Gold selling album within three months of its release. Forty years on, Rockin' the Fillmore is still a classic live album and showed us why Humble Pie and (in particular) Steve Marriott (who tragically died in a house fire while smoking in bed at the age of 44 in 1991) still sounds fresh today.  RECOMMENDED!

0439023521|5.0|****SPOILER WARNING****  I understand how hard these books are to digest. Yes, pitting teenagers to the death and expecting the victor to recover from the horrible two weeks of trauma in that arena is very upsetting (especially when a little twelve year old dies). But it is much like a soldier who comes back from war and is expected to live life normally again. It wouldn't surprise me that they would somehow gain a little bit of PTSD. If you interpret the books further and look past the blood, nudity, and deaths, it is actually a very compelling series about how corrupt human society has become. We (or they) think problems can be solved by law enforcement in the act of violence. The Hunger Games (from the glorified interviews to cold-blooded killings) is almost like a war in a way.  War is bad. There's nothing in the world (ex: ideas, point of views, censoring) that will completely hide the fact that it is bloody, horrific, and all-in-all negative. Positive outcomes or solutions may come out of it, but war in itself is, well, bad.  Yet how Suzanne Collins presents this futuristic situation is ingenious in a very upsetting manner. We all cringe whenever a tribute dies (either it's Cato or Glimmer... or Thresh and little Rue) because we think of how that person could have lived past their 18th year. How they never reached their 20s and were never able to live, have a family, and mature and grow old. And I believe that because she chose children to kill each other instead of adults, we notice how much more unjust the Capitol and President Snow really are. Someone with empathy and compassion for others would see how atrocious this is, yet someone as heartless and selfish as the Capitol people or President Snow would see only the pure entertainment and the vengeance they receive from it.  With all the injustices in these books, I noticed a positive theme that was floating around since the beginning page of the Hunger Games to the last page of Mockingjay: Hope. Hope for survival (and to keep the promise for Prim) was Katniss', and Peeta's, deep-down determination throughout the 74th Hunger Games, even though they kept denying they would win. Hope was evident in Catching Fire with Katniss talking with Thresh's and Rue's family. Also the pearl that was lovingly given to Katniss from Peeta was a symbol of hope for her, along with the dandelion flower. The whole reason for the war in Mockingjay was for the hope that future generations didn't have to live through what past ones did. And in the epilogue, Katniss thought of hope as she watched her children playing in the Meadow, for she knew they would have brighter futures than she ever had. Isn't that what parents strive for? For their kids to have better opportunities, better chances, better knowledge, so they could achieve the greatest?  Yes, the book may pose heart-wrenching themes, but there even throughout all these injustices, all these survivors can still hold on to hope and happiness, even when they've lost their right mind.  Many books I have read had characters put into very similar situations with deaths and wars, but I believe that the authors never truly understood how to apply those real-life reactions to their imagined characters. But with Suzanne Collins, she comprehends it and expresses it in a very powerful way in her novels. It even compelled me to sit and think about how unjust war and murders were.  I highly recommend this entire book series, not just The Hunger Games, for anyone who loves a good story with a deeper, much more psychological meaning in between the lines.

B00571F26Y|5.0|First, this is the most fascinating, informative, and convincing diet/health book I've read in recent years.  I won't get into an exhaustive discussion of content, as others have done that, but here's the book's thesis in a nutshell: Dr. Davis posits that wheat products are the single biggest cause of weight gain, body fat, and a host of other diseases including heart disease and diabetes, and backs it up with research and studies.  I can't believe I'm saying this about a health book, but this was actually a page turner - it's quite convincing and gripping.  This is one of those books where you will find yourself saying \\"wow\\" every five minutes.  Now, a few minor caveats.  First, while he mentions it briefly, Davis doesn't emphasize strongly enough that removing wheat must be combined with other dietary restrictions.  For example, you can't remove wheat, but then gorge on sodas, sugary snacks, and ice cream and expect to lose weight and be healthy.  Wheat is a certainly a major factor, but it's not the only piece of the puzzle.  Second, a valid argument could be made that this book is simply another tome advocating low carb eating.  However, Davis is more convincing and thorough in his argument than others I've read.  Third, removing wheat products from your diet is a JOB in my view.  It's everywhere - at the office, at parties, barbecues, etc.  A tremendous, almost inhuman amount of discipline is required in my humble opinion unless you're a total recluse.  Despite these minor negatives, the book is so well written and researched that I'm sticking with five stars.  Highly recommended for the health conscious reader.

B003Q6D25K|5.0|Paranormal Activity 2 is the sequel with really no improvements whatsoever.  However because I love the series, I can't help but give it a perfect 5-star rating anyway.  I honestly, truly believe anyone who's a fan of the Amityville Horror (the original one) will LOVE the Paranormal Activity series.  Alright, so Paranormal Activity doesn't necessarily wrap a storyline around these paranormal events, but then again, it's not really supposed to-  it's all about building horror and finding the creepiest ways imaginable to get underneath the sensitive side of the viewers.  So why isn't the sequel better than the first one?  Well a couple reasons.  Now we have children.  Two children- one a teenage girl; the other a baby who spends most of his time in a crib.  We also have a German Shepherd always on alert.  PLUS the parents and the wifes sister.  Yes, with all these people involved, you can't possibly have a movie deliver with the suspense in the same way as the first one.  The simplicity of the setting and including only two people (a young couple) makes the first one much scarier.  Also, another reason it's not as scary- the camera angles.  Whereas on the first Paranormal Activity the camera is mainly locked into one specific spot in the bedroom where the man and woman sleep, now the camera has a few other permanent positions, such as the baby's room above the crib, the living room/kitchen area, the swimming pool outback, and the hallway near the front door.  This means whenever the tension is about to build, the cameras alternate between all four of these areas.  I prefer the way it was done in the original.  That way we'd learn to appreciate and grow accustomed to the setting and the frightening scenes as our eyes absorbed into the darkness of the bedroom waiting anxiously for the next horrific scene.  With a camera switching between four different areas of the house, now there's not as much build-up in the suspense.  On a similar note, it's amazing how brilliant the horror aspect works in a movie like this.  It's so simple and yet, a genius idea!  You learn to understand how the camera system works- when everything gets quiet and we return to a familiar camera position, you *know* something scary is about to happen, but there's never any telling what the actual scary moment will BE.  It could be anything!  There's literally hundreds of ideas the movie writers could have implemented.  I love it!  Actually a third reason this isn't as scary as the first one is because now we know what to expect.  Honestly the movie writers should have kept this in mind because the first 3 or 4 paranormal scenes are VERY predictable and remarkably tame in comparison to even the mildest ones from the first Paranormal Activity film.  I don't want to spoil what they actually are in case you haven't seen them yet, so just know the BIG shocks won't appear until later.  Oh well- at least the movie doesn't take a while to get going this time around- you only have to wait 15 minutes until something unusual happens, whereas in the first one you had to wait 30 minutes.  Now onto the positives-  I have both Paranormal Activity 1 and 2 on DVD, and part two has *drastically* louder and better sound overall.  Honestly the first one is quite mild in the sound category, so you have to make constant adjustments on your volume button.  With part one, I recommend just turning it up louder than you normally would and leave it be.  With part two... you should probably turn the volume down a few notches.  With naturally louder volume, when the unexpected horror segments arrive, get ready to be REALLY scared!  I mean downright shocked!  I love it!  Overall however, the amount of terrifying segments just aren't as numerous as in the first film.  Plus it seems like, with the parents, there's a \\"been there, done that\\" feeling for those of us who've watched the first Paranormal Activity movie (especially with the wife and how the evil lurking in the house takes over her mind and body).  I'm not particularly fond of the setting- San Diego once AGAIN!  Let me share the same sentiments again (in case you haven't seen my review for the first Paranormal Activity that I submitted a couple weeks ago).  San Diego is the wrong location for a horror setting.  It's too mild there.  A much better place would be the northeast, especially anywhere from Massachusetts down to Georgia.  Overall, expect a fantastic horror movie, but don't be surprised if it doesn't live up to your expectations, and definitely don't be surprised if it doesn't live up to the superior part one.

B005URRCUY|5.0|I'v listened to every Black Keys album so far and have loved every minute of every one of them. They seem to be some of the few artists out there where it's obvious that they make music for the simple enjoyment of it and haven't gotten caught up in everything else fame and fortune has to offer after 10 years of playing.  That said, in regards to the Black Keys fans who don't like all of the Black Keys' albums: The BK don't make their albums the same way that most artists do. Most artists are in a constant process of song writing/performing, where they'll write a song, shelve it, and pull it out for their next album or wait for the right album to put it on. The Black Keys make their albums in a time span of about a week, writing and playing songs for the first few days, then selecting the tracks with the most potential before editing the songs and ordering them on a CD. This means that each album has no forethought or planning, making each release a snapshot of the band in time, and each album having a tone and mood very different from the next.  So, for those of you that don't like this album and are asking the Black Keys to return to their roots, I say \\"not gonna happen.\\" This is what makes the Black Keys special. Besides, the sound that this band has isn't what makes them great, it's Dan Auerbach's super-creative and resonant guitar riffs and lyrical genius and Patrick Carney's aggressive and soulful drum-beating, which as far as I'm concerned has been prevalent in every BK release to date. El Camino is no exception, with a groovy 60's vibe coupled with Dan and Pat's aforementioned skills, this CD does not disappoint and has stayed in my CD player since I bought it 2 months ago.

B005M5OSBK|2.0|This Pink Martini album continues the tradition of eclecticism that Pink Martini seems to build on. I would characterize this album as follows: most songs taken individually are enjoyable well done, with China Forbes's vocals really admirable, but the way these styles are intermixed and left in a random way definitely speaks to a lack of artistic vision. One can, and should, use style heterogeneity as a means of artistic expression, but in Pink Martini's case this mish-mash seems much more like a feeble excuse for an inability to find the band's own identity. Contributing to this impression are several songs which cannot be described otherwise than eerie (\\"Que serra serra\\", \\"Moon, river...\\") and, speaking plainly, act just as filler and are disappointing to the listener. As it is the message of the album remains unclear other than that a bunch of musicians just got together and played a number of random songs. The inside flap that covers the screen is a bit flimsy, but if you're not rough with it, it should last you quite a long time. It also has a nice felt-like surface on this inside so that it doesn't scratch your Kindle screen, this was an excellent touch to the overall product. The screen flap also has a crease down the middle to allow it to bend in half so that it can hold the Kindle upright while sitting on a flat surface. Over time, I believe this crease may crack and break apart, but if you don't use the crease to prop the Kindle up alot I wouldn't worry too much.  This case also has a stretchy band that allows the front flap to be held in place to protect the device while it is not in use.  The manufacturer also took into account where the Kindle's speakers/power buttons etc. are located and allowed the case to not cover these areas.  This was an excellent and cheap buy for my wife's Kindle, we're both quite happy with it.  Pros:  -Cheaper than other cases out there.  -Tough case, with a teflon/non-slip grip feel to it.  -Inside of screen flap protector has felt-like cloth to prevent screen scratches.  -Well designed - case doesn't cover speakers/power buttons etc.  -Stretch band that secures front flap against screen while not in use.  Cons:  -Cheap, front flap crease may be prone to wear and tear over time.  -Teflon surface catches all finger grease, easily removed though.  All in all a very great, cheap, cover for the Kindle Fire! Worth the buy.

Julia, the journalist, starts to investigate the Vel d' Hiv roundup in 1942. B0057O6IMS|4.0|Was it wrong that I was pulling for the annoying kid to remain trapped in the basement? Basements are inherently scary places. Allow the Godfather (and, occasionally, Maestro) of Gore, Lucio Fulci, to illustrate why. THE HOUSE BY THE CEMETERY came out in 1981 but it hasn't lost its vitality or its ability to generate them shudders and spine-tingling chills. Here, Fulci's affinity for the lurid is on full exhibition. But when is it never?<br /><br />THE HOUSE BY THE CEMETERY opens with a shot of Daniela Doria who you may remember as having literally puked out her guts in CITY OF THE LIVING DEAD. This time something else comes tearing out of her mouth, namely a sharp blade which had entered by way of the back of her skull. Poor Daniela Doria, the eternal victim on celluloid. The camera then shifts to the Boyle family as they relocate from New York City to a forbidding oak mansion in New England. Not that this was a deal breaker, but there's a macabre history attached to the Boyle's new home, what with its most recent resident having hung himself. And when Lucy Boyle (gorgeous Catriona MacColl) makes mention of the graveyard next door and that strange tombstone in the hallway, her husband (Paolo Malco) brushes away her concerns. \\"This ain't New York,\\" he scoffs. No, not even a terrifyingly tenacious bat can dissuade this guy. Maybe someone should've paid heed to the freaky little psychic girl who keeps warning people not to go into the house.<br /><br />This has been repeated ad nauseam: Lucio Fulci is an exciting but flawed filmmaker. Caught between either coherence or sensationalism, he always went with the flash and the show, sacrificing script and internal logic. Once again, here's a film that finds Fulci champing at the bits, eager to get to the scary set pieces. Ghoulish innuendo and creaky noises and the establishing of plot points can only sate him for so long. Gorehounds will be well pleased with Fulci yet again. He treats us to gruesome decapitations and prolonged scenes of graphic stabby stabby. Fun fact: A blade can be cruel to human skin. Somewhere, the creators of SAW may have gone \\"Hmmmm...\\"<br /><br />The acting is negligible, although credit Catriona MacColl for playing \\"Lucy Boyle\\" with such nerve-jangling fragility. The English dub is mostly passable. The one exception is the dubious dubbing of the little Boyle boy which is so aggravating that even the actor who played the boy is compelled to apologize in the DVD bonus interview, assuring his fans that that wasn't his voice (\\"Sorry, sorry, don't hate me for that.\\").<br /><br />THE HOUSE BY THE CEMETERY is part ghost story, part slasher flick. Fulci makes good use of the haunted house score and ominous camera compositions to build up unbearable tension. The big bad, when finally revealed, is worth the wait as he looks absolutely menacing. And, of course, he lurks in the basement. Basements (and cellars) are, to reiterate, scary places. They're meant not as much to store fine wine but rather to provide a venue to conduct terrifying, inhumane experiments. It's the sort of place in which a fella can comfortably say things like: \\"He needs human victims... to renew his cells. It's how he stays alive.\\" The ending is typical Lucio Fulci, meaning that it is dark and bleak and unsettling. It puts the punctuation on a nifty little horror cult classic.<br /><br />This is the Special Edition DVD. Note that you can set up the audio either in English or Italian (although the boy is less grating on the ears in Italian), with sub-titles available in English, French, or Spanish. The bonus features are:<br /><br />- \\"Meet the Boyles\\" - Interviews with actors Catriona MacColl (still gorgeous) and Paolo Malco (14 minutes)<br />- \\"Children of the Night\\" - Interviews with actors Giovanni Frezza and Silvia Collatina, who played the kids in the film (12 minutes)<br />- \\"Tales of Laura Gittleson\\" - Interview with actor Dagmar Lassander (9 minutes)<br />- \\"My Time with Terror\\" - Interview with actor Carlo De Mejo (9 minutes)<br />- Deleted Scene - Bat Attack Aftermath (without audio and lasting about 30 seconds)<br />- International and U.S. Trailers<br />- TV Spot<br />- Poster & Still Galleryw with actor Carlo De Mejo (9 minutes)<br />- Deleted Scene - Bat Attack Aftermath (without audio and lasting about 30 seconds)<br />- International and U.S. Trailers<br />- TV Spot<br />- Poster & Still Gallery

1576875776|5.0|I'm surprised how many one, two and three star reviews there are for this excellent photo book.  On any level it really can't be faulted.  Blank pages in a photo book are quite common though here the confusion was probably caused by having them very close to each other and repeated as such throughout the pages.  Book designers would normally space a few blanks roughly evenly between all the photos.  The printing by Asia Pacific is comparable to any other quality printed art book (and it costs less than high-end printing in Europe or the US).  The duotones use a 200 screen on a very acceptable 157gsm matt art with perfect register.  The slight black/ochre feel to these photos, I thought, was a reasonable color choice.<br /><br />As to Vivian Maier's photos, the 106 in the book are quite stunning.  In particular the framing is just prefect and I think this separates her work from other well known street photographers especially those classed as the New York School who go for a more emotional style of imagery.  The photos reveal everyday street scenes in Chicago and New York and work so well because they always pull you into the composition without any distractions about what to look at first.  The pacing helps here too, close-up portraits merge into medium shots and then, perhaps, a long shot saturated with detail.  The more I looked through these pages the more the sequencing added to the photos.<br /><br />If I have a criticism it is that there is really nothing about Vivian Maier.  Her named website gives some background and I think it would have been helpful to the reader to have included some of this material<br /><br />John Maloof, the book's editor, is to be congratulated on finding and preserving a treasure trove of photos by an unknown photographer who had such a remarkable eye.  I'm hoping this will be the first of many books revealing Ms Maier's creative vision.

I often find his novels though are longer than necessary and excessively mundane at times and I find myself wishing that his editors would trim at least a hundred pages from most of what he's written. Sarah promised to come back for him as soon as she could. However, it took much more time than she expected. This led to a very tragic ending.  Sarah Starzynski, a girl born in France was brutally arrested by the French police. She was desperate to save her four-year-old brother Michel, so she locked him in the cupboard and kept the key. Sarah never gave up hope while in the concentration camps. Sarah always fought to escape the camps, and rescue her brother. She was a very hardworking and kind-hearted girl. While in the camps, Sarah regretted locking Michel in the cupboard. She always wondered if she would eventually be able to escape. When Sarah returned home, she was devastated with what she discovered. Her brother had passed away.  On the Vel' d' Hiv's sixtieth anniversary, Julia Jarmond, an American journalist, was asked to investigate the roundup. It is now the year of 2002 and Julia then learns all about Sarah's past life. As Julia investigates Sarah's life, she begins to question her own life. She begins to reevaluate her marriage and her romance. As Julia digs deeper and deeper into Sarah's life, she becomes very intrigued by her story. She begins to feel as if it is her mission to figure out what truly happened.    If you are looking for a dramatic and exciting book that you will never put down, this is your perfect choice. Sarah's Key includes a little romance and suspense, which makes it very intriguing. I would highly recommend this book to whoever is looking for an emotional and dramatic novel. If you are in middle or high school, this book will teach you a lot about the tragic history of France. This book is very educational, but at the same time it will have you sitting on the edge of your seat. I personally enjoyed this book very much, and am glad I had the chance to read such an inspiring novel.

B0001L3LUE|5.0|This documentary is especially compelling for anyone who lived through the 1960s.  McNamara, after all, was the quintessential establishment man and one who had supreme self confidence.  The best modern-day comparable I can think of is Rumsfeld, though McNamara was a lot smarter and more subtle.  Can you imagine Rumsfeld telling all and being confessional in any respect?  The same holds true with McNamara -- which is why seeing him acknowledge mistakes and life lessons is so jarring.  Morris' camera technique, which prompts McNamara to look right into the lens, is a stroke of genius.  The pain in McNamara's eyes is what makes the movie entrancing, and makes one want to watch it again.<br /><br />McNamara acknowledges the evil of the fire bombing of Tokyo in which he participated as Curtis LeMay's aide, but defends the measure as necessary for doing the ultimate good of ending the war.  He deplores war, but is realistic enough to acknowledge its inevitability and gropes for rules to limit its incidence and effects.  His discussion of the Cuban Missile Crisis is chilling, and is confirmed by recent studies of archival material from the old Soviet Union.  The military pushed for a preemptive raid, and had Nixon won in 1960 that's exactly what we would have done.  But Kennedy's reluctance to do so is vindicated by evidence that Soviet field commanders had tactical nuclear weapons and the authority to launch them into the U.S. mainland.  The raid would have provoked World War III.  McNamara credits a former U.S. Ambassador to the U.S.S.R. for the famous decision to ignore Krushchev's second belligerent telex and respond to his first more accommodating message. McNamara deplores how close we came to nuclear war in the 1960s and acknowledges the simple chilling truth that very rational people are still capable of extraordinarily irrational acts.<br /><br />On Vietnam, McNamara portrays himself as advising Kennedy on the un-winnability of the war and the need to withdraw all advisors.  Kennedy agreed, though McNamara acknowledges that such a decision is always in flux and capable of revision given the politics at work.  He believes that Kennedy would never have escalated the way Johnson did.  But McNamara does not give any convincing explanation for why policmakers felt compelled to escalate and why he did not take better advantage of the resources that were deployed.  On this latter point, Westmoreland and his war of attrition methods have been the subject of much crticism, which Morris does not touch on in his interview. 0143039431|3.0|The difficult lives of the Joad family during the Great Depression of the 1930's are very relevant to the America of today. The transition of the 1930's economy in the Midwest to mechanization is similar to the current transitional economy in the U.S., where we are moving from manufacturing to more of a tech base. We are moving away from what we know to something we don't know, and people are not sure what to do or what to make of it.<br /><br />I have loved the Steinbeck books I have read up to this point (Travels With Charley, Of Mice and Men, Tortilla Flat, and Cannery Row), but Grapes is much too one sided for me. In typical Steinbeck fashion he puts you right into the story, but he paints with much too broad a brush here. It is slanted, and does not give equal time to both sides. The bad is too bad and the good is too good. Steinbeck seems to leave out a basic understanding of economics, perhaps intentionally, and I'm afraid lots of people just swallow the anti-corporate message without thinking. Some think companies are an unending fountain of money. They are not. Education is the problem, as it was then and continues to be.<br /><br />The Grapes of Wrath brought to light many things that needed changing in the United States of the 1930's, and for that I give it credit. Illegal monopolies, unfair labor practices, corrupt local officials, etc. are all effectively criticized here by Steinbeck. The immigrating Joads, as legal U.S. citizens, have rights as citizens which should be protected.<br /><br />The character of Al, one of the Joad's sons, is a bright spot. Al is changing and embracing the future by learning to work on cars, even after the mechanization has forced the Joads out of their sharecropped farm. The Great Depression forced people to move and to change, developing new skills as the economy changed. Al's forward-thinking adaptation made me wonder if the government relief mentioned in the book simply enables people to sit right where they are when things change and simply cash a government check each month, draining society instead of adapting to the change and contributing to the new economy in a positive way. I am afraid this type of thinking so prevalent in modern society is teaching people to become dependent on the government (in exchange for votes) instead of going out and making their own opportunities.<br /><br />While reading The Grapes of Wrath I lost patience with Steinbeck's rhetoric in which corporations and the wealthy got that way by unjustly taking advantage of others. Even small businesses (restaurants, stores, gas stations, etc.) in the book are treated with contempt. Small businesses create jobs. Large corporations create jobs. Individuals who gamble their family's security and future by starting their own businesses create jobs. These are positive forces in the economy. The Government taking money away from these positive forces is a drain, which must be justified in one way or another.<br /><br />At times the Joad family is starving, but they balk at working for wages they deem are too low. You do what you have to do however, and the Joads end up working and eating. Companies don't get ahead by cheating people for very long, and The Grapes of Wrath effectively illustrates the labor abuses of that time. Reforms were made and things eventually got better for the workers as well as for the economy as a whole. It is how the Free Market works, for better or worse. The real challenge lies in dealing with the transitional economy and figuring out how to prosper in it. It is a cycle, and The Grapes of Wrath is timely today because we are in that same type of economic cycle in the U.S. right now. Rather than looking to The Grapes of Wrath for answers, however, maybe we should look to it instead to learn from our past mistakes.government check each month, draining society instead of adapting to the change and contributing to the new economy in a positive way. I am afraid this type of thinking so prevalent in modern society is teaching people to become dependent on the government (in exchange for votes) instead of going out and making their own opportunities. B005MYEQ4U|4.0|I love Woody Allen.  He has such a delightful way of crafting his comedic adventures into something so thoughtful and provoking, without ever feeling heavy handed or manipulative.  He allows his characters to speak for themselves and their situations to come alive within a realm of awkward realism.<br /><br />`Midnight in Paris' has many of the same qualities that make Allen's filmography so impressive, but it lacks some of the internalized charm I was hoping for.  The performances are mostly uniformly great, with a few standouts, and the overall feel to the film is certainly effervescent in the way that most `great' Woody Allen films are, but there is a filmy layer of forcefulness that I didn't expect nor did I truly love.  Instead of allowing us to take from this film what we needed to take from it, Allen told us what we needed to glean, thus taking a little away from the film's actual impact.<br /><br />In `Midnight in Paris', we are introduced to a young couple on the cusp of marriage.  They are drastically different individuals.  Inez is self-centered and condescending and materialistic and Gil is a dreamer with his head in the clouds and his eyes set on a fantasy idea of what life should be like.  Inez and Gil accompany Inez's parents to Paris for a business venture.  Inez is more concerned with eating at posh restaurants and buying overly priced lawn furniture, while Gil just wants to soak in the beauty of the city.  Gil is obsessed with the past, the nostalgic feeling of a time lost to him because he is simply not content with what he has.  He is convinced that yesteryear was so much better than the time and place he dwells in now.  While spending time alone, the clock strikes midnight and Gil finds himself transporter to 1920's Paris, a time he often fantasized about living in, where he meets the likes of Fitzgerald and Hemingway and soon falls for a beautiful woman who has a thing for tortured artists.  Living in this fantasy deep into the early morning hours and then struggling to connect with his fianc&eacute;e during the day, Gil uncovers the truth about his wanderlust and begins to understand his relationship with Inez for what it really is.<br /><br />A mess.<br /><br />The acting is pretty good.  Owen Wilson basically plays Woody Allen and he does so with mixed results.  His soliloquys are somewhat misdirected and feel awkward, and not in the comedic way that Woody pulls them off, but he really captures the awestruck feelings thrust upon Gil.  Rachel McAdams plays a pretty horrible person, and she does so with a finesse was wasn't expecting.  Michael Sheen is a clich&eacute; and not as funny as I wanted him to be.  Marion Cotillard is mesmerizing as the alluring love interest for Gil, but it is Corey Stoll who steals the whole show as the boozing, self-obsessed, prolific Earnest Hemingway.<br /><br />My issues with `Midnight in Paris' lay with the script, which is surprising since it's Woody Allen and it's been winning award after award.  The script pushes Gil's personal views in a way that are consuming.  It is the only viewpoint expressed and it is done so rather heavy handed like.  The other thing that bothered me was that everyone in Gil's life was so different than him, so awful and unlikable that it made zero sense that he was even living the life he was living.  How did he end up with these people when he hates them and they obviously abuse him too often?  I also found parts of the film lazy, especially when you consider how Gil comes across a certain diary.<br /><br />Alas, Woody still knows how to create an air that is engulfing, and his recreation of 1920's Paris is charming to the hilt.  Whenever Gil is dwelling in that world, `Midnight in Paris' is a total keeper.  This just feels like two films trying to live cohesively, and it can't wholly.ing to connect with his fiance during the day, Gil uncovers the truth about his wanderlust and begins to understand his relationship with Inez for what it really is. I have a new found respect not just for Apple and Pixar, but for this man and his drive for integrated marriage of art and technology.  This book does uncover fundamental flaws in certain personality traits, but also illustrates a good man--a good human being--who's passion for his genius ideas shows us who he really was.  On a final note, when I read the one and two star reviews, i wondered if some of these folks actually finished the book  If you only read halfway through you'll have a very different perspective of this man than if you read the entire book cover to cover.  In my opinion, it's written very well with a balanced and unbiased perspective.  It isn't until the very end that Isaacson actually  opens up with some personal opinion.  This is the way it should be written--leaving the reader to make up his/her own mind.

There are differences, too, and they are poignant:  the strong family bond, the lack of governmental aid to the poor, and how they lived, from their cars to their houses both simplifies and complicates the Joad's journey to California and they hope, stability for their family.  Anyone who has driven along Route 66 or the Texas panhandle, for instance, can really appreciate Steinback's intense description of the Joad's journey to California and just how nerve-wracking it must have been without the benefit of a good car, motels, bottled water, and credit cards.<br /><br />For those who might complain that the narrative intermingled with the Joad's story is too lefty or smacks of propaganda, I think it adds a good balance to the story. 0307592731|2.0|I was interested in reading Cheryl Strayed's yet unreleased memoir mostly because I spent most of my life along the central spine of Southern California and enjoyed numerous summers hiking and camping through much of the same area she wrote about. With a picture of a single weathered and worn-down hiking boot on the cover and a title like &#34;Wild (From Lost to Found on the Pacific Crest Trail)&#34; one would expect to be reading a book about the author's nature-based adventures in the great outdoors of our westernmost border states. And to a minor degree that is what some of this story is about. It wasn't until I read the Amazon.com promotional piece on this book that I realized the title wasn't just referring to the Pacific Crest Trail (PCT), and very possibly not at all. It could very well be the author's one-word self-portrait. According to author Cheryl Strayed, she wrote &#34;Wild&#34; relying primarily on her journal notes, which I don't recall her mentioning in the story, and her own recollections of this journey in 1995. The reader should keep in mind that this book wasn't finished until 17 years after the author completed her journey.<br /><br />Strayed's memoir pictures a passionately independent 26-year-old woman from Minnesota whose life had been so much less than what she had hoped for, some of which resulted from her less than satisfying family life as a child, but mostly from her own poor life choices. Not long after several family tragedies, including the self-inflicted demise of her marriage and alienation from her siblings, Strayed makes what can only be described as a most bizarre, and to some of her friends a potentially suicidal decision to fly to the West coast and backpack her way up at least the majority of the sometimes treacherous Pacific Crest Trail (over 1,100 miles!). Why? According to Strayed, she needed to get away to think about her life, her past tragedies and her future. For whatever reason, backpacking for hundreds of miles all alone on the PCT, with no real trail experience to speak of, and only a nominal knowledge of how to use her equipment in an over-sized pack too heavy to use safely, was evidently the first thing that occurred to her. This, in itself, gives the reader an inkling about Strayed's thought processes and disjointed sense of reality.<br /><br />SPOILER ALERT: There may be plot information beyond this point that some readers may not want to know. If so, stop now or continue reading at your own risk.<br /><br />Here is a more detailed view into Strayed's mindset and insufficient level of preparation for her PCT adventure in her own words:<br /><br />&#34;It was then that I remembered that guidebook I'd plucked from a shelf at REI [A wilderness supply store of sorts] while waiting to buy the shovel a couple of days before. The thought of the photograph of a boulder-strewn lake surrounded by rocky crags and blue sky on its cover seemed to break me open, frank as a fist to the face. I believed I'd only been killing time when I'd picked up the book while standing in line, but now it seemed like something more--a sign. Not only of what I could do, but of what I had to do. When Aimee and I reached Minneapolis, I waived her off at her exit, but didn't go to mine. Instead, I drove to REI and bought &#34;The Pacific Crest Trail, Volume 1: California&#34; and took it back to my apartment and stayed up reading it all night. I read it a dozen times over the next months. I got an abortion and learned how to make dehydrated tuna flakes and turkey jerky and took a refresher course on basic first aid and practiced using my water purifier in my kitchen sink. I knew I had to change.&#34;<br /><br />As you may see, when Strayed makes a decision it is rarely well thought through or well planned, and she rarely consults anyone else but herself. Most of her decisions are reactionary and of the knee-jerk variety. If you doubt this, just take a peek at her self-designed PCT preparation &#34;To-Do&#34; list: Let's see . . . Divorce my husband . . . Check! Pack condoms . . . Check! Shack up with some guy of questionable character, have sex, sex, sex and mix in some cocaine while I'm at it . . . Check! Have an abortion . . . Check! Blame all my failures on my family . . . Check! Learn how to prepare dehydrated food . . . Check! Pick up and move over 2,000 miles away from Minnesota to somewhere in the desert near the the boarder of California and Mexico . . . Check! Pack a huge backpack so full of stuff until it's well beyond my capability to safely carry it . . . Check! And then, when that's all done, backpack and hitchhike over 1,100 miles along the Pacific Crest Trail . . . Check! Does anyone else see a dangerously self-absorbed pattern here?<br /><br />Early on in this book Strayed's major focus is on her mother's looming death. She incessantly wrote about how great the loss of her mother would be to her life. Yet, during one visit to her mother's hospital room Strayed's attention was not on her dying mother, but was instead fixated on the &#34;bulging crotch&#34; of a male nurse, who by the way was tending to her mother's care, while Strayed was deep into her fantasy of dragging the nurse into the bathroom for &#34;Wild&#34; sex. What does that tell you about this so-called grieving daughter?<br /><br />To some small degree, Strayed does attempt to come to grips with her &#34;Wild&#34; lifestyle and bring under control her incessant habit of putting the blame on other people and other things for her problems. In her own words:<br /><br />&#34;I knew what he [Her husband, Paul] was thinking. The way he looked at me told me everything I'd refused to hear from Lisa. I was different. I wasn't there. Heroin had made me that way. And yet the idea of giving it up seemed impossible. Looking Paul squarely in the face made me realize that I couldn't think straight.&#34;<br /><br />And later in the book, she wrote:<br /><br />&#34;And what Clarke knew and Rogers knew and what thousands of people who preceded and followed them knew. It was what I knew before I even really did, before I could have known how truly hard and glorious the PCT would be, how profoundly the trail would both shatter and shelter me.&#34;<br /><br />And then again near the end:<br /><br />&#34;What if I forgave myself? I thought. What if I forgave myself even though I'd done something I shouldn't have? What if I was a liar and a cheat and there was no excuse for what I'd done other than because it was what I wanted and needed to do? What if I was sorry, but if I could go back in time I wouldn't do anything differently than I had done? What if I actually wanted to [****] every one of those men? What if heroin taught me something? What if yes was the right answer instead of no? What if what made me do all those things everyone thought I shouldn't have done was what also had got me here? What if I was never redeemed? What if I already was?&#34;<br /><br />Even with what I have already said, the reader can be assured of a few redeeming qualities in this book. Strayed is an exceptional writer and storyteller, I can't emphasize that enough. So you shouldn't have a problem zipping right through this 300+ page memoir. That is, if you can also just zip over and around the crude language, her obsession with sex, her own body and a few other less than admirable personal traits that I won't mention here. Also, be prepared for a lot of repetition in this story. Some facts that are covered in the first few chapters are brought up again and again, as if the author thinks they may have slipped the reader's attention the first, second or ever the tenth yime they were mentioned.<br /><br />Once I finished &#34;Wild,&#34; I paused to consider why Strayed had been so repetitive. One thing that struck me was regardless of her obvious need for others, she seemed to so strongly relish being alone; she mentioned this over and over while on the PCT. It was then that I realized that characteristically Strayed is an unscrupulous opportunist; so much so that it made her nonsensical choice of a solo hike up the PCT make some sense, at least in a very childishly bizarre way. It gave her the most independence and at the same time the greatest opportunity to control her relationships, as if simply hiking away from them would be considered an admirable thing to do to those who had so selflessly helped her along her hike. But that is the way Strayed is.<br /><br />I've chosen to leave out one graphically violent scene in this book, mostly because it made no sense at all and I have no idea why it was even included. And also because it has already been written about in some detail in a number of other reviews of this book.<br /><br />Considering all of the other nature-based things she had to deal with on the PCT, Strayed's endless commenting on the death of her mother wore so thin that her alleged emotional bond with her mother became less believable as I moved through the book. I'm sure most readers felt her grief and guilt, though often very gratuitous, quite early on in the story and didn't need to be reminded about it several times a chapter.<br /><br />To a great degree, this book amounts to little more than Strayed's overindulgence in her unseemly personal fantasies. In her Author's Notes at the beginning of the book, Strayed (Which by the way is her &#34;acquired&#34; name. You will have to read the book to understand that one.) indicated that some material was pulled from her own recollections and some of her characters had been modified. She also goes on to say that some of the people and events were simply omitted because she deemed them unnecessary to the veracity of the story. Well, after reading this book, it is my opinion that there are still quite a number of people, events and language that could also have been scrubbed from &#34;Wild&#34; which also would not have adversely affected the veracity of the story either; especially if Strayed is the least bit interested in reaching the broadest possible audience.id, before I could have known how truly hard and glorious the PCT would be, how profoundly the trail would both shatter and shelter me.&#34;  And then again near the end:  &#34;What if I forgave myself? B002G1YPH0|5.0|First and foremost I'm cheap and not ashamed to say it.  I bought this because the fan that came with my stock cooler was making an annoying whine noise so it had to go.  This cooler was pretty much bar none the best value for the money, and in quite a few tests out performed coolers double its price.  I really had no intention of over clocking my processor until I got this cooler and saw how big of a drop I had in idle temps.  If I remember right I dropped about 10c at idle and at least that much with prime95 running my 1100T at 4.0 ghz.  That right there makes it worth it to me.  I added a second 120mm fan I had just sitting around.  It doesn't match the fan that comes with the cooler at all, 1100 rpm vs the 2000rpm.  Even with the slower fan as the pull and the stronger one as a push I still saw temps drop a few degrees again.  If you don't plan on over clocking or don't live in a hot environment the one fan is all you'll ever need.  With the fan at its full 2,000 rpm it is still a heck of a lot quieter than my graphics card.  I think the fastest I saw it run without manually setting it faster was 1800ish rpm.  Most time though it was just whispering along at 1000-1200 rpm, pure awesomeness.  So for those of you who are worried about noise, don't be, my dad's case fans on high are still louder than this stock fan on high.  Install was probably the biggest headache for me.  I tend to over analyze things and try to figure everything out before hand.  Which sometimes just leads to too much thinking and though and not enough doing.  The youtube videos I found didn't really help me and the instructions were worthless.  Best advice is to glace at the instructions, watch a couple of the youtibe videos and then just do it.  It's on there now though and that's where it's going to stay, at least until I upgrade again.  I have a cooler master 932 full tower and this thing fits with inches to spare.  Don't know if it will fit yours but measure twice cut once is normally a good rule of thumb.  For all those who say that this cooler will block one of your ram slots, yeah it can especially if you have tall ram.  I have corsair's dominator ram that comes with heatsinks on the ram.  With the heatsinks on the fan wouldn't fit on the ram side.  I had 4 options:  Remove a stick, remove a heatsink from a stick, do just 1 fan pulling (which seems to be the popular answer), or do what I ended up doing just offset the fan.  Simple solution to a simple problem.  Yeah so my fan doesn't sit flush with the top of the cooler's heatsink, it comes out about 1\\" above the top of the heatsink.  With the push/pull setup I didn't notice any temp differences.  So if you want you can try that solution instead of leaving out a stick of ram.  Even with just one fan I can't really see it harming anything other than aesthetics.  Pros:  -Dropped my stock temps quite a bit and helped keep my over clocking temps well below that dreaded 70c \\"limit\\"  -Cheap  -Quiet with the fan that came with it  -Easy install of fan and second fan  -Works as advertised  -Easily customizable for any 120mm fan.  I currently have 2 120 scythe fans that my buddy gave me (2 of these spinning at their max 3k rpm can wake the dead)  Cons:  -Install can be a big PITA, varies per person but general consensus seems to learn towards PITA  -Uhhh...won't cook me bacon?  not really a con but price has gone up six bucks in the less than a month since I bought it.

Don't get me wrong, I did enjoy this book and it would make a GREAT movie.  How about releasing it on 11/22/13, fifty years after that fateful day when I was 10 years old and heard the news on TV from my sickbed and thought I was dreaming.
1608193381|1.0|Upon reading the table of contents of this book, I knew from the start what the world view of this author was - statism pure and simple.  His socialistic tendencies show through each chapter especially those where he speaks of \\"shoulds\\" and \\"oughts.\\"  These are words of elitism from past decades and centuries.  This is Maxism dressed up in modern clothing.  Nothing new here.<br /><br />The author is simply a copy cat of old, failed, and stale economics that have never worked and never will.  He looks to government control to make decisions for those whom he says can't make decisions.  To this author, the general population is unable to decide for themselves.  The shadows of Thomas More and Thomas Hobbes have reappeared in this book - an elitist class deciding for all the paupers and minions who own nothing and look to this elite ruling class to provide them with everything.<br /><br />This author doesn't realize that Keynes is dead and will never resurrect.  He also doesn't realize that it was government that caused the latest big recession and not the business cycle.  Greenspan betrayed his own principles through manipulation of interest rates, giving a false message to business for investment in their projects, thinking there was sufficient capital.  Reno and Clinton forced banks to lend to uncreditworthy people and popped the housing market.  Obama made it worse with his \\"stimulus\\" packages that left the rivate sector wilting while his cronies and unions made off with government money.  He is also doing the same thing Clinton did, fining banks for not lending to people who can't afford a mortgage.  This beckons for another bust.  Yes tell us again about your 23 things they don't tell you about capitalism.  Each statement is loaded with faulty premises and \\"shoulds\\" and \\"oughts\\" much like Marxism of bad days gone by.<br /><br />ADDITIONAL REVIEW<br /><br />This is a fresh review that tackles some of the more massive errors and logical fallacies with Ha-Joon Chang's book 23 Things They Don't Tell You About Capitalism.  I will take the time to address the salient points in his book that not only demonstrate that he is a very poor writer but also that his knowledge of capitalism generally and the Austrian School specifically is subpar or worse.  Consequently, his argument begins on a weak foundation.<br /><br />This book caught my attention because I believe and participate in the free market capitalism that Chang claims does not exist.  Yes, Mr. Chang, there is free market in spite of you pronouncing it dead.  I will address this in due course in my analysis of his book.<br /><br />THE NEBULOUS \\"THEY\\"<br /><br />From the title of his book onward, Chang discusses this plural \\"they\\" without being specific about whom he speaks.  In reading the Introduction, it seems that this \\"they\\" could be referring to \\"free-marketeers.\\"  Then again, Chang cites no particular economist or professional (except perhaps Milton Friedman, and Friedman is not the sum of the proponents of free-markets.  Far from it!), which leaves this \\"they\\" hanging there in space without any reference whatsoever.  Since this is the case, Chang begins his long string of logical fallacies, the first being the straw man.  He sets up this straw man at the beginning of each chapter and starts kicking it around with perplexing eyes looking on wondering exactly what he is doing.  However, all his kicking at this straw man is simply beating the wind.  In any critique when using a third person designation, sound writing principles dictate that there should be a reference.  However, this does not stop Change from such poor writing.<br /><br />BIFURCATION OF FREE MARKETS AND CAPITALISM<br /><br />In attempting to show he is not anti-capitalist and that free markets (as he understands it) do not exist, Change creates a bifurcation of free markets and capitalism.  He writes, \\"The book is not an anti-capitalist manifesto.  Being critical of free-market ideology is not the same asbeing against capitalism...I believe that capitalism is still the best economic system that humanity has invented.  My criticism is of a particular version of capitalism that has dominated the world in the last three decades, that is, free-market capitalism\\" (Introduction, xv).<br /><br />This bifurcation demonstrates his ignorance of capitalism and that he has never read the Austrian School economic theories.  Where he gets this bifurcated version of economics is nowhere noted in his book.  The reason for its absence is that such capitalism does not exist.  He invented this straw man so he could create a bifurcation.  Without free markets, capitalism would not exist.  Capitalism depends on the freedom to own, create capital, produce goods and services from this capital, and exchange property within the economy.  Capitalism derives from capital, that is, the basis for productivity.<br /><br />George Reisman, a very close friend of Ludwig Von Mises and many other Austrian economists, describes capitalism as, \\"...a social system based on private ownership of the means of production.  It is characterized by the pursuit of material self-interest (NOT selfishness) under freedom (my italics)...Based on its foundation and essential nature, capitalism is further characterized by saving and capital accumulation, exchange and money...the profit motive, the freedom of economic competition...economic progress, and a harmony of the material self-interests of all the individuals who participate in it\\" (Economics and Capitalism, p.19).  Free markets is the bedrock of capitalism, but Chang's ignorance of economics prevents him from recognizing this.  He cannot get to capitalism without beginning with free markets.  Therefore, if he is anti-free market, he must necessarily be anti-capitalist.<br /><br />NO SUCH THING AS FREE MARKETS?<br /><br />According to Chang, free markets do not exist (Thing 1).  How does he know this?  He says so.  To him, these \\"free-marketeers\\" only came about during the 1980's.  In his dreams they did.  What he does not realize that free markets have been around as long as humanity has been on the earth, and it has not disappeared.  As long as people own property, create capital from it, and conduct transactions, freedom exists in the exchange of goods and services.  The government may attempt to place restrictions on freedom through taxation and regulation, but humanity's ingenuity shines the brightest when this happens.  They discover means of creating free markets as Adam Smith discussed concerning the early centuries of Great Britain.  The only way to wipe out free markets is through absolute dictatorship and totalitarianism with no recourse of it ever returning, which makes all peoples paupers.  That has never happened in the course of human history.  Different dynasties attempted this, and their dynasties disappeared.  However, Chang's straw man of his version of free market prevents him from recognizing history.<br /><br />THE SHOULD AND OUGHT MENTALITY<br /><br />Chang engages in an elitist mentality by using \\"should\\" and \\"ought\\" (Thing 3).  In his thingy 3, his title informs us of his proscriptive anti-capitalistic ideology when he states outright that \\"Most people in rich countries are paid more than they should be.\\"  Oh really!  Who determines this?  What he then writes does not even support this conclusion.  One example is that \\"most workers in rich countries could be, and would be (italics mine) replaced by workers from poor countries.  Really?  Says who?  Chang?  There is no discussion of division of labor, skill, and supply and demand.  It will just happen.<br /><br />He then tells a story about something being more expensive lends to it being \\"better.\\"  This he launches against free market capitalism on the basis of price differentiation between different societies rather than on the basis of the activities of free markets, competition, and wealth creation.  Chang ignores all of the principles of capitalism that reflect reality and fails to even discuss them. He provides no discussion or analysis of supply and demand, capital creation, or competition and how these lead to growth and wealth creation.  Rather, he simply sets up his straw man of his idea of \\"free markets\\" and viciously attacks it, using bifurcaton to splice free market from capitalism to create his idea of capitalism that has no basis in the free exchange of property or the liberty of property ownership.  Since he bifurcates these two concepts, he has no basis for capitalism.  It just hangs out there like fruit without branches to hold it.  This is complete nonsense.  His story of Sven and Ram ignores market principles.  It has to, because if Chang admitted that these principles existed, they would demolish his straw man \\"free market\\" ideology.  Supply and demand in competitive free markets determine product and service value and drive prices down not up.  However, Chang's blinders prevent him from understanding these economic principles so evident in free market capitalism.<br /><br />CONCLUSION<br /><br />I could comment on each subsequent chapter and the nonsense he discusses in each, but the above examples demonstrate that Chang knows little of principles of economics and has no understanding of free markets or capitalism.  He hangs up numerous straw men and begins whacking at them.  One of these straw men is Adam Smith, one whom Chang said assumed the worst of individuals and set the stage for that mentality to be passed on.  Chang quotes Smith out of context (Thingy 5) and disregards that The Wealth of Nations actually arose from a previous work The Theory of Moral Sentiments in which Smith elevated individuals by positing in them the high qualities of ethics, virtue, liberty, and individual and private rights.  Chang does not understand Smith or his works.  I doubt that he ever read Smith.  Consequently, he misunderstands Smith's self-interest principle and invisible hand but rather accuses him and those who follow him of being selfish.<br /><br />What he does not realize is that these straw men are concoctions of his own imagination and have nothing to do with the real world.  Chang's book has a place...in a trash heap.out during the 1980's.  In his dreams they did.  What he does not realize that free markets have been around as long as humanity has been on the earth, and it has not disappeared. She is an enigmatic person who the reader, her boss  Armansky, and Blomkvist,  her future work companion, have a difficult time figuring out. What motivates this character? What happened in her past? These are questions that all three would like to know as the book proceeds. She is one of the best fictional characters ever invented, and makes the reader put up with Larsson's plodding pace. B0016OLC5Q|5.0|Sean Penn's adaptation of the adventures of Christopher McCandless, who left society behind to aimlessly roam the western United States, find himself and try his hand at a life off the land in the remote Alaskan wilderness. At first preachy and morose, there's a tipping point early in the picture where I couldn't help but feel something for McCandless and his naive quest to subsist wholly on morals, philosophy and the kindness of strangers in place of tangible food and water. Presented as a sort of messiah figure, the errant youth touches the lives of a broad range of individuals over the course of his travels, preaches the gospel of live-and-let-live and uncovers the difficulties associated with the tramp's lifestyle in a society so concerned with protecting its citizenry from themselves. There's a lot to be learned from Christopher's path, both positive and negative, and Penn shies away from neither in this careful, convincing pseudo-documentary. It can be so enveloping at times that the sudden appearance of a recognizable actor or two along the way feels awkward and strange. A very solid effort with something to say, I only wonder if it may have been better served by a full cast of unknowns. Eddie Vedder's perfectly matched original soundtrack deserves a special mention.

0312576463|5.0|I purchased Jonathan Franzen's \\"Freedom\\" in a fit of desperation.  After struggling to connect with \\"Corrections\\" and abandoning it after a hundred pages, I scoffed at the praise and love \\"Freedom\\" received when it first came out over a year ago.  In a writing class I was taking I described Franzen as my generation's most overrated writer, saying, \\"He's not bad, he's just no great white hope.\\"  Now, I can gleefully feast on my own words.  I did not buy \\"Freedom\\" because I wanted to.  I have been traveling and burned through the books I brought with me.  I staggered, desperate for entertainment, into an Italian bookshop and muttered \\"English books?  Any?\\"  My question was met a vague wave towards a small corner that carried dull reproach.  It was \\"Freedom\\" or a John Grisham book.  I almost opted for Grisham.  \\"Freedom\\" is a frustrating book.  The characters in it are largely unlikable, not because of any false characteristics, but because of their blistering humanity.  I cringed at Patty and Walter and Joey not because I did not believe them, or believe in them, but because I recognized the honesty in each oft them.  I could see pieces of me, of my friends and family, in all of Franzen's creations.  \\"Freedom\\" is brilliantly written, and Franzen's prose has a flow unlike anything I've read in a good long while.  Still, the book is punishing in many respects because of how poorly these characters treat each other.  Again, I could only think of the people in my life and how, despite their best intentions, they harm each other constantly.  Franzen's novel is a brilliant representation of a firm belief of mine: it does not take a bad person to be a bad person.  In that, these people-- characters seems light here, for you get such a complete and total overview of Franzen's creations that they really seem like people-- are not monsters, they are horribly, disgustingly human.  They forget man's incredible capacity for harming those who he loves.  They struggle, they rise anddwindle.  They hurt.  Up until the last fifty pages of \\"Freedom\\" I liked it greatly but found it to be too crushing to say that I loved it.  Franzen does something incredible in finishing off his novel.  He finds beauty, genuine beauty, in the muck and mire of the pain we, as humans, inflict.  So, yes \\"Freedom\\" is tough at times and rough in its unflinching portrayal of just how petty and sad people can be, but it is also a complete triumph.  It is a book that justifies literature in a time of easy temptation and distraction.  I could not recommend it more highly.

These songs are Leave Out All The Rest, Shadow Of The Day, What I've Done, Valentine's Day, In Pieces, and The Little Things Give You Away. Leave Out All The Rest is my favorite track on this album. A lead singer in a band seriously can't be screaming, \\"Shut up when I'm talking to you!\\" or, \\"The very worst part of you... IS ME!\\" for seven years, or at this rate, twelve years of his life. On top of that, the other band members can't showcase their aggressive side for that long either.<br /><br />So, this is all I have to say about the particular album. It gets bothersome to people who are actually willing to see this band grow as musicians and as people. Limp Bizkit has also gotten this kick-in-the-behind by their older fans when their 2003 album Results May Vary was released. Another band that has also gotten this criticism is A Fire Inside (AFI). I read Nate McCooey's review of this album and he mentioned the same band. Seriously, America, when are we going to stop being this stupid? Oh well, it doesn't matter anyway. B005URRCUY|5.0|Those of you old enough to remember the days when cheap cassette tapes asked your forgiveness for their poor quality will get the joke behind the \\"PLAY LOUD\\" sticker on the Black Keys' new disc.  The rest of you...now you know why \\"El Camino\\" sounds the way it does.  Produced by Danger Mouse, El Camino sounds cheap and you love it for that.  The high ends are a bit too harsh, like Alice in Chains' album \\"Dirt\\", the music isn't antiseptically-clean, and the whole package is a perfect match for the Black Keys' style.  This is alt-rock that borrows from the '60s, '70s, and '80s and is unashamed about it.  It has wailing vocals that seem to land somewhere between Queens of the Stone Age (as on \\"Lonely Boy\\") and the bellow of young Bono (as on \\"Dead and Gone\\").<br /><br />Probably the most addictive tune on this album has to be \\"Gold on the Ceiling\\":  it thumps into your ears with a padded-drum beat and fuzzed-out keyboards that mimic The Doors at their analog best, then gives way to a clap-along beat you can't help but want to move to.  The guitars bang against the high registers like the Beatles' \\"Revolution\\" and from time to time there's a Phil Spector \\"Wall of Sound\\" style echo to the chorus.  \\"Little Black Submarines\\" has a lo-fi Chili Peppers ballad sound to it.  Vocalist Dan Auerbach sounds at times as forlorn as Jack White...which fits because then halfway through it breaks out into a wash of distorted guitar rock and cymbal-heavy percussion.  When you get moments like this, it's as if your ears become convinced that everything you've ever liked about rock was just thrown into a blender and someone set the buttons to \\"Liquefy\\".  There is something for everybody here.  Like The Cult?  \\"Money Maker\\".  Need some heavy folk-rock with just a tinge of stoner?  \\"Run Right Back.\\"<br /><br />In trying to convey El Camino to you, I've just hopped across a basket of bands and quite a few decades.  That doesn't mean this album is a derivative mash-up, though:  think of it more as a genre piece.  Danger Mouse recently did an album (\\"Rome\\") that borrowed a kind of spaghetti-western sound--for The Black Keys, this is the same kind of thing:  a rock album pressed through a retro-filter that's come out the other side a guilty pleasure.Danger Mouse recently did an album (\\"Rome\\") that borrowed a kind of spaghetti-western sound--for The Black Keys, this is the same kind of thing:  a rock album pressed through a retro-filter that's come out the other side a guilty pleasure.

B006P8KKYW|5.0|Trent Reznor and Atticus Ross took an unusual approach to scoring The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo:  they made the score *before* the movie was filmed.  A lot of moviemakers like to keep a stock of what they call 'reference music':  tunes that to them evoke the feel of the movie they're trying to create.  The music stays with them and helps keep them in the appropriate frame of mind.  When Reznor and Ross were asked to help assemble some reference music for The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo, the reference music they made ended up being the final product (I'm sure with an adjustment or two).<br /><br />\\"Immigrant Song\\" opens Trent Reznor and Atticus Ross' score to the newer \\"Girl with the Dragon Tattoo film\\" phenomenally:  it is intense, Karen O's caterwaul is chillingly good, and the music seems to comes straight out of the future, despite it being a fairly faithful cover of the decades-old Led Zeppelin song.  I absolutely love this tune, but the rest of the album is completely UN-like Immigrant Song.  Instead we get a simply beautiful series of musical passages.  They are ambient, often dark...and they make great background music for just about anything.<br /><br />If you're familiar with Nine Inch Nails, there was a side EP to \\"The Downward Spiral\\" called \\"Further Down the Spiral\\", which contained alternate versions and other material that didn't end up in \\"The Downward Spiral\\".  \\"Further Down the Spiral\\" showcased Trent Reznor exploring more atmospheric sounds, in some ways similar to the soundtrack for the original Quake game.  Fast forward to recent history and Reznor again broadened his instrumental palette with the haunting and aptly-named \\"Ghosts I-IV\\".  If you are familiar with any of these works, this score really does an amazing job of playing across those sounds.  It is not always as laid-back as \\"Ghosts I-IV\\", but it also never quite sounds as heavy as \\"The Downward Spiral\\"--it's out on the fringe where \\"Further Down the Spiral\\" was forged.  There are fewer strings than \\"Ghosts I-IV\\", with the piano taking the center stage for most of the tracks.<br /><br />What amazes me is how much Reznor and Ross seem to move between 'full' and 'minimal':  \\"Hidden in Snow\\" is barely there, but has this thrum of bass under the surface that slowly builds the tension.  On the other hand, \\"An Itch\\" is bubbling with activity.  Apart from \\"Immigrant Song\\", the only other standout track is the closer \\"Is Your Love Strong Enough\\" (featuring How to Destroy Angels).  Probably the weakest track on the album, it sounds like it went at the end because it got played during the movie's closing credits.<br /><br />If you're already a fan of Nine Inch Nails, you will only regret buying this album if you didn't care for \\"Ghosts I-IV\\".  If you're new to Trent Reznor's musical style and liked the way it sounded in the movie, this music can go with you just about anywhere and still stand on its own.  The score to \\"The Social Network\\" is also like this--if you liked that music, this is even better.  If you enjoy the score to The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo, you should look into the Nine Inch Nails album \\"Ghosts I-IV\\" and even consider \\"The Downward Spiral\\" or \\"The Fragile\\".ts I-IV\\", with the piano taking the center stage for most of the tracks. This particular book is a great opportunity though to read it aloud with a 10 year old child and therby being present to answer any questions. Wikipedia's online glossary of nautical terms would also be a helpful reference for a pre-teen reader while reading this book. By all means though i highly recommend this attractive and brilliantly illustrated book as an excellent learning experience for kids.
