0307949486|2.0|**Slight spoilers**<br /><br />Like with most popular, well-publicized books, you usually get two parts of the majority: the ones who love the book and proclaim it genius and those who can't stand the sight of it and think it rubbish. I'm of the minority, I suppose. I didn't love or hate it.<br /><br />It's an okay book to waste several days reading. I mean, it certainly interested me enough to where I lost track of time for hours just reading away. The main plot, the Harriet Vanger mystery, while following the usual mystery outline--complete with the typical cast of suspects and even the bad guy revealing at the end their nefarious deeds and the how/why to the protagonist--I actually found it nice enough. I can't say I was surprised with most of the revelations, but I think that part of the book was nicely handled for a typical mystery, overall. I also did like the twist of the hero getting captured and having to be saved by his female partner, who embodied more of what is stereotypically the male hero's characteristics.<br /><br />I know some people saw Larsson as a misogynist because of the atrocities that befall some of the female characters (what? Two?) in this book, but I don't share this opinion. Quite the opposite, actually; both of those characters rise above what happened to them and actually are stronger than the male characters in the book. Simply because someone writes about certain things does not mean they condone it. In fact, I think Larsson felt strongly about abuse of women and wanted to bring it to light. Sure, some parts of this book are manipulative and clearly just there for the shock value (the ludicrous rape of Salander for the second time was done for no other reason in my mind). Yes, the author's ideas and views could have been handled a bit clearly (it seems sloppy at times), but I don't think the book suffers that badly for it. Then again I'm of the mindset that even the silliest plots can be pardoned as long as there are worthwhile characters, but sadly...<br /><br />The characters in and of themselves weren't particularly great. Blomkvist was bland and uninteresting. I wouldn't call him an author insert, directly, but you could easily tell that a lot of what he said and felt in regards to certain topics were all really Larsson; his protagonist was just the mouthpiece. Blomkvist seemed like a nice enough guy, though, but he was just so damned boring. Why did Salander fall in love with him? He was nice and didn't judge her, but that's also a basis for a good, solid friendship. I just couldn't see how she, or anyone for that matter, would fall hard for such an uninteresting, bland man. As for Salander... Something about her smacked of trying too hard to be edgy and original. I think Larsson went a bit overboard. There were times, however, where I did find her pretty damned badass. I just think her character could have been better balanced. The other characters aren't really worth writing about here because they weren't really fleshed out.<br /><br />Anyway, the book was a nice enough read. I may even reread it again in the future. But I doubt I'll buy the two remaining books in the series; I just don't find the characters interesting enough to warrant it. I want a book to stick with me for some time after its end and this book was simply forgotten after I read it.<br /><br />**1/2he characters in and of themselves weren't particularly great. B0000542D2|5.0|One of the great marriages-of-style in movie history, the Beatles' music and Richard Lester's direction are a knockout combination. A Hard Day's Night is purportedly a busy-day-in-the-life of the Fabs as they make their way to London to appear on a TV show, with hilarious consequences.  Screenwriter Alun Owen gives the boys a very good foundation, and the allegedly \\"broad stokes\\" he took to portray them (sarcastic John, charming Paul, sardonic George, put-upon Ringo) IMHO seem unforced and inoffensive.  It's characterization, that's all, and in the early days the Beatles did look very much alike, so the \\"broad strokes\\" aren't so much exaggeration as helpful to the odd viewer who doesn't know the Beatles that well.<br /><br />What really gives the movie its kick is the boys' genuine, broadly-smiling enjoyment at what they're doing (especially during the numbers \\"I'm Happy Just to Dance With You\\" and the reprise of \\"I Should Have Known Better\\", where the usually sullen George for no apparent reason suddenly breaks into a silly little dance while playing his guitar).  Director Lester captures the boys at all the right moments in many exceptionally well-lit and well-set-up shots during the songs.  Basically he sets out to make the Beatles photograph as attractively as possible, and he succeeds.  The Beatles had a remarkably photogenic, bisexual sort of appeal, most notably in their early days, as captured so well here.<br /><br />The later Beatles movies, Help, Magical Mystery Tour, Yellow Submarine, and Let It Be all have moments of brilliance, but Hard Day's Night is by far the strongest, most accessible, most likable and polished of the lot.  Funny, beautifully shot, extremely well-edited, all these years later Hard Day's Night has lost none of its power to set your toes tapping, your body grooving, and just leaving you feeling immensely better in general.  Yeah, yeah, yeah, yeeeeaaaah!

0061122416|2.0|What brought me to The Alchemist was the continual badgering I got from friends telling me that this was an amazing book that would be perfect for me.  I finally caved.  I found it and read it.  What I got out of The Alchemist was a badgering lecture on metaphysics.  Don't get me wrong, I can see where Coelho is coming from in wanting to write a spiritual guidebook for people without direction to find their way in the world, to follow their dreams and if they follow them deep enough that their dreams will come true.  All-in-all The Alchemist is a very longwinded Biblical-esque Ali Baba fable which seems to exist in a place out of time.  Aesop could have done it more justice in three paragraphs.  In fact, The Alchemist would be better, as short as it is, if it were made even shorter, cutting down on the poorly drawn characters who stood to represent types rather than individuals.  Coming from someone who has pondered what he wants out of life and who he wants to be when he grows up I suppose this book might help a fifth grader to follow his superstitious notions into whatever dark recesses of the cosmos he should fall into.  But based in reality, this book and it's story is not based in reality.  People whether we like it or not, change.  The characters in this are, all of them, too stubborn to do anything but the one thing they are used to.    The writing is nothing to shout about.  If I never have to hear Soul of the World or Personal Legend ever again I will be just fine.  Perhaps the translator is to blame.  The one thing I wished he'd come clean and just shouted out was the Franco-American motto: Pursuit of Happiness.  What upsets me is that people, after they have read this, consider themselves to be well read.  Instead of putting in hours with Dostoevsky, who would offer a much more compelling tale of morality.  At least the reader would gain some actual perspective about what the character is truly thinking, instead of being paranoid to death of omens.  The Alchemist proves to really be an oasis for lost souls.  But if you have read this and you are ready to move on, try something like Cat's Cradle by Vonnegut, to give you a much more entertaining and enlightening view of hokey religious gobledy gook that The Alchemist takes far too seriously.  Where Coelho succeeds is in leaving a stench of self-assumed self-serving self-importance of his self-proclaimed philosophy which, I'm pretty sure, Pinocchio and Walt Disney and a billion other people throughout history has already stated and stated it in a more enjoyable way.
